Health care in the U.S. is undeniably expensive, but how bad is it really? According to National Geographic, this graphic effectively captures the three key issues: cost, outcomes, and accessibility. For those unfamiliar with such visualizations, here’s a brief explanation: Countries are plotted on a graph with annual per capita health care spending on the left and average life expectancy on the right. Lines connecting these points illustrate the relationship between spending and health outcomes. Ideally, you'd want an upward trend, indicating better outcomes for higher spending. However, the U.S. doesn't appear on the chart, suggesting poor value for money. Additionally, the graphic highlights access issues: thicker lines represent more frequent doctor visits. Countries like Japan and the Czech Republic have easily accessible health care systems, whereas the U.S. falls short in this regard.

Does feeling connected within your community truly matter, given that you have connections elsewhere, say online? Kim and his team were unique in their approach by controlling for individual social connectedness. They also factored in dispositional elements, such as optimism, alongside demographic and health variables including age, race, income, marital status, education, mental health, and risk factors for heart disease like diabetes, obesity, and hypertension. When I inquired about encouraging community engagement, Kim remained cautious, stating, "There isn't sufficient evidence to recommend it. This is merely a correlation, not causation. But it surely can't hurt." I envisioned the interaction differently: "Hi, I’m your neighbor from downstairs," "Oh, hello. I've heard you playing music sometimes," "That's great, I often sing while I dance," "It's wonderful! Could you teach me?" "Not today, I’m a professional dancer," "Of course, work-life balance is key. I just wanted to introduce myself because knowing my neighbors is good for my health." "Really? Come in, I was planning to go to the gym, but let's chat instead." "Perfect, we'll both reduce our heart attack risks." "Wonderful." "I'm Adam," "I'm Evelyn, or Eve for short. Nice to meet you, Adam." "Nice to meet you, Eve. It's been a pleasure." Kim posited that the cardiovascular benefits observed might stem from neighbors checking in on each other, offering support, sharing resources, and fostering a sense of community vigilance. "As a psychologist," Kim noted, "I strongly believe that emotional support can mitigate the negative impacts of stress." Positive psychology, which emerged after traditional psychological research focused mainly on dysfunction, explores the benefits of optimism and a sense of purpose. Kim and his team have examined how positive traits influence health outcomes, finding that heightened optimism correlates with a lower risk of heart failure and stroke.

Prior to environmental laws and regulations, loggers extensively cut down forests, hunters decimated wildlife, and companies discharged toxic waste into water supplies. Here’s a look at how the U.S. appeared before these protections. **Fire-prone waters** Before the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) was established, the Cuyahoga River frequently burst into flames due to oil, sewage, garbage, and industrial waste, which killed fish and sometimes ignited, according to the EPA. The most severe fire occurred in 1952, yet the river continued to be used for dumping waste. The final fire in 1969 prompted the Clean Water Act and the formation of the EPA. **Polluted waters** The Clean Water Act aims to safeguard American drinking water from pollution by mandating companies to use cleaner technologies in production processes, reducing waste. **Air pollution** Visible smog, a mixture of smoke and fog, results from car exhaust, coal burning, and other pollutants, harming lung function and causing allergic-like reactions. Recent U.S. regulations, such as those governing factory emissions, seek to decrease air pollution and mitigate smog. **Toxic waste** In 1980, the EPA initiated the Superfund program to address hazardous sites where industrial companies had disposed of toxic substances like lead and arsenic, contaminating air and groundwater and posing health risks to nearby residents and ecosystems. **Berkeley Pit example** Since the late 1800s, mining operations in Montana dumped waste into nearby streams and wetlands, contaminating soil, groundwater, and surface water with heavy metals including aluminum, arsenic, mercury, and lead. The EPA has been addressing this site since the late 1980s but continues to manage ongoing risks. **Deforestation** Before logging laws, large areas of land were stripped of their ancient trees. The National Forest Management Act of 1976 helped preserve forests from overharvesting. **Endangered species** American bison nearly vanished due to hunting, but now survive in small numbers thanks to government protection. The Endangered Species Act enables authorities to safeguard threatened plants and animals.
TORONTO – Toronto police have apprehended a Brampton man in connection with Sunday's TD Bank robbery in which two individuals were injured by gunfire. Inspector Mike Earl reported that forensic evidence at the scene guided the police to one of the two suspects wanted for the bank robbery. Police are still searching for the individual who shot a teller during the incident. Security footage shows the suspects—both wielding a handgun—leaping over the counter at a TD bank branch on St. Clair Avenue near Runnymede Road around noon on Sunday. A customer tackled the second suspect as he leaped back, and the first suspect fired at the customer, striking a 22-year-old teller in her thigh. The 54-year-old customer pursued the suspects into the parking lot, where he was shot in the abdomen; the teller is expected to recover. The customer underwent surgery to remove his spleen, part of his pancreas, and a portion of a kidney and is currently hospitalized. Earl advised the public to avoid intervening and cooperate during such situations, stating, "If someone demands your money or watch, comply to prevent further harm." However, he noted that the suspect had a loaded firearm, indicating premeditation. Bank employees undergo training to prioritize safety, as the primary goal is public and employee safety, not the protection of money. The suspects fled with an unspecified amount of cash, with some believed to have been rendered unusable due to a bank security device. Police located a stolen green four-door Honda Civic, sought in the case, in the Jane Street and Wilson Avenue area on Sunday night. The Canadian Bankers Association is offering a reward of up to $100,000 for information leading to the arrest and conviction of the suspects.
AUSTIN, Texas — Daily-use batteries might soon become more affordable, compact, and lightweight. Researchers at The University of Texas at Austin's Cockrell School of Engineering have identified a family of anode materials capable of doubling the charge capacity of lithium-ion battery anodes. Consequently, batteries found in devices ranging from smartphones to large-scale energy storage systems could become more efficient. The newly discovered family of anode materials, named Interdigitated Eutectic Alloy (IdEA), simplifies the production process by reducing it to just two steps, as opposed to the multiple steps typically needed for mass-producing lithium-ion battery anodes. The researchers produced a foil material that is one-quarter as thick and half as heavy as the graphite and copper anodes used in virtually all lithium-ion batteries today. This innovation could enable the creation of smaller, lighter rechargeable batteries in the future. "We've developed an economical, scalable method for creating electrode nanomaterials," stated Arumugam Manthiram, a professor and director of the Texas Materials Institute, who led the research team. "Our findings demonstrate that this material excels in performance metrics essential for a commercially viable advancement in lithium-ion batteries." Recent attempts to enhance lithium-ion battery electrodes have involved constructing new nanomaterials atom by atom. Manthiram and his team, comprising postdoctoral fellow Karl Kreder and materials science and engineering graduate student Brian Heligman, devised a new class of anode materials where eutectic metal alloys are mechanically rolled into nanostructured metal foils. Since the 1990s, the primary anode for mass-produced rechargeable lithium-ion batteries has been a graphite powder coated on a copper foil. The copper adds unnecessary weight without enhancing the battery's power, and the anode requires a complex, intricate manufacturing process. By eliminating the complicated slurry coating process, the production of the IdEA anode becomes significantly simplified. Kreder, the lead author of the study, recognized that a micrometer-scale alloy anode could be converted into a nanomaterial using conventional metallurgical alloying techniques. "The eutectic microstructure forms naturally due to thermodynamics," Kreder explained. "Subsequently, you can reduce this structure through rolling, which is an extremely cost-effective way to transform a microstructure into a nanostructure." This research, published in ACS Energy Letters, addresses a significant hurdle in commercializing improved batteries for portable electronics such as smartphones and medical devices, as well as larger applications like electric vehicles. While you can't control lighting or backgrounds in photos, you can influence other aspects. Blue is widely considered flattering on most people, so wearing shades of blue, green, or purple can enhance your appearance. However, colors like yellow or red might be trickier to pull off and could negatively affect your look depending on your skin tone. The position of your face—whether you tilt your head or look directly at the camera—is crucial, but ultimately, the photographer is best positioned to guide you. For casual social media photos like those on Facebook, focus on analyzing your existing photos to identify the angles that work best for you and replicate them in future shots.

On March 8, 2012, a logo of consumer goods giant Henkel was featured before its annual press conference in Düsseldorf. Reuters reported that Henkel, a German consumer products company (HNKG_P.DE), had agreed to acquire U.S.-based laundry and home care firm The Sun Products Corp., valued at $3.6 billion, including debt, from a fund managed by Vestar Capital Partners. This acquisition would integrate Sun’s laundry detergents All and Sun, and fabric conditioners Snuggle into Henkel’s portfolio, positioning Henkel as the second-largest laundry care player in North America after Procter & Gamble but ahead of Church & Dwight. Berenberg analysts noted that leveraging Henkel’s innovation capabilities on Sun’s brands could significantly enhance margins beyond expected synergies. Despite a 0.6% decline in Henkel’s share price to 1045 GMT, reflecting broader market concerns due to Britain’s decision to exit the EU, Henkel remained among the top performers on Germany’s DAX index. Adding Sun Products’ business, with annual sales of approximately €1.4 billion in the U.S. and Canada, would boost Henkel’s North American sales contribution from 13% to about a third. Assuming an operating margin of around 12%, Berenberg anticipated the acquisition to contribute about 6% to Henkel’s operating profit in 2017, with this figure rising to 17% by 2019 through revenue synergies. Baader Bank’s Christian Weiz valued the deal at roughly 2.3 times Sun’s sales, deeming it highly attractive and affirming his “buy” rating on Henkel’s stock. Sun Products employed around 2,000 people across two U.S. manufacturing sites and a research and development center. Financial advisory roles were handled by Perella Weinberg Partners and Credit Suisse, while legal counsel came from Cleary Gottlieb Steen & Hamilton LLP. The transaction was fully financed through debt and underwritten by Deutsche Bank, JP Morgan, and BNP Paribas.
NVIDIA timed their announcement perfectly, revealing native support for 2 and 3-way SLI on Intel X58 motherboards after I left NVISION 08. While this was certainly a significant development, there’s no bitterness on my part. The big news today is that NVIDIA has introduced a certification program to enable SLI on X58 boards, ensuring compatibility and generating additional revenue. Any X58 motherboard manufacturer can apply for certification, which involves paying a fee to NVIDIA. If approved, the board receives a key to enable SLI natively through the BIOS. When installing the NVIDIA drivers, the system checks for this key; if present, you gain SLI support. However, this feature is exclusive to X58 motherboards for upcoming Nehalem processors. This development is groundbreaking since it allows users to enjoy both SLI and CrossFire capabilities without needing specialized hardware like OEM systems or Skulltrail. Supported configurations include two or three GPUs, with PhysX acceleration also available for 3-way SLI setups. For maximum bandwidth, 3 PCIe x16 slots require the use of nForce 200 chips, while alternative slot combinations are available. Although the nForce 200 route comes with higher costs and power consumption, it remains an option.
People frequently inquire about the salaries in Formula 1, a topic that is quite complex due to the calculation of the total earnings based on the Formula 1 group's annual earnings (EBIDTA). While it isn't entirely transparent what components are included or excluded, the basic distribution is known: Ferrari receives 5% directly, 2.5% goes to the teams' share, and another 2.5% to the commercial rights holder (CRH). Ferrari's allocation is justified by its status as "The Longest Standing Team." The prize fund is split into two columns. Column 1 is distributed equally among all qualifying teams (teams that finished in the top 10 for two of the last three seasons), while Column 2 is apportioned based on performance, with World Champions receiving 19%, followed by the remaining nine teams in decreasing percentages: 16%, 13%, 11%, 10%, 9%, 7%, 6%, 5%, and 4%. Additional bonuses include the Constructor Champions' Bonus (CCB), rewarding the three teams with the most race wins over the past four seasons—2013, 2014, 2015, and 2016. Despite Mercedes winning 54 races, Red Bull 18, and Ferrari just five, the CCB focuses on team hierarchy. Currently, Mercedes receives $37 million, Red Bull $33 million, and Ferrari $30 million, translating to $685,000, $1,830,000, and $6 million per win, respectively. Teams that win consecutive Constructors' Championships receive an additional $30 million annually, which has significantly benefited Mercedes, who have won three championships. Adding sponsorships, merchandising, and licensing revenues, it's evident that Ferrari doesn't heavily invest in F1 marketing—other than through a few theme parks and retail sales. Therefore, the notion that leaving F1 would save money for Ferrari is inaccurate; instead, it would require substantial investment to maintain its market presence.

She noted reports of nearly 100 deaths and dozens of injuries in Damascus from January 21 to 26. Additionally, the club signed its first Designated Player, Panamanian striker Gabriel Torres, to a long-term contract. The National Symbols Officer of Australia recently contacted Juice Media, creators of Rap News and Honest Government Adverts, alleging that their use of the Australian coat of arms in their videos contravened several Australian laws. Despite these videos being clear examples of satire, with no reasonable person mistaking them for official content, the coat of arms in question was even misspelled. It's regrettable that the government struggles to differentiate between impersonation and satire. This concern is heightened given the proposed legislation that threatens jail time for impersonating government agencies. While some anti-impersonation laws, such as those in the U.S., are justified against fraudsters posing as tax officials, the Australian proposal lacks adequate safeguards. The recent letter to Juice Media suggests the government might misapply the law. Australian Lawyers for Human Rights argues in a parliamentary submission that the proposed legislation is overly broad. For instance, the clause imposing a two-year sentence for impersonating a government agency doesn't demand intent to deceive or actual harm. Such provisions risk penalizing activities beyond the intended scope. Although the bill includes an exception for "genuine satirical, academic, or artistic" conduct, critics argue this leaves room for government interference in satire. Additionally, the "solely" condition for satirical purposes might deter speech. Is a satirical video unprotected if it also aims to generate advertising revenue? Similar misunderstandings of satire have occurred elsewhere. In 2005, a Bush administration lawyer accused The Onion of legal violations over its use of the presidential seal, though The Onion maintained its work was unmistakably parody. The White House wisely chose not to pursue the matter. If it had, it would likely have faced First Amendment challenges. Without a First Amendment or written bill of rights, Australian civil libertarians fear the impersonation law could stifle political commentary. We hope the Australian government either rejects the bill or revises it to mandate intent to deceive and provide stronger protections for satire. The Delhi government aims to boost the city's green cover from 20.08% to 25% over the next five years. This requires adding around six to seven hectares of green space in the capital, which is currently facing severe pollution. Various government departments, including the Airports Authority of India, municipal corporations, and the Delhi Development Authority, will convene in April to outline an action plan. To achieve this, the Department of Forests and Wildlife plans to designate about six to eight meters on either side of upcoming elevated road projects for green belts. Shukla noted that when the right-of-way is approved for these projects, the horticulture department must ensure these areas are densely planted. Additionally, the government intends to plant trees along drainage channels and in vacant plots managed by the DDA. Shukla also mentioned that 42 city forests will be developed by constructing pathways and installing solar lights. Third parties, such as community councils or student-led environmental task forces, will help implement and evaluate these initiatives. "We aim for public involvement to make sure the plan isn't just on paper," Shukla emphasized.

The Navajo Nation (Photo: Daily Times stock image) – A federal court judge has confirmed that the Navajo Nation can proceed with its lawsuit against Urban Outfitters Inc. under the Indian Arts and Crafts Act. Filed in February 2012, the lawsuit accuses Urban Outfitters of trademark violations, unfair competition, and violating the Indian Arts and Crafts Act. The complaint cited the sale of items like clothing, jewelry, and accessories labeled as "Navajo" on Urban Outfitters' website and in stores. These products were removed in October 2011 after the tribe sent a cease-and-desist letter. On December 21, U.S. District Judge Bruce D. Black ruled that the tribe meets the requirement of an "injury in fact" needed to pursue claims under the act. Urban Outfitters argued the tribe lacked standing due to a lack of injury. Black's 10-page decision dismissed both parties' summary judgment requests. Enacted in 1935, the Indian Arts and Crafts Act aims to safeguard Native American cultural property, and an amendment in 1990 allowed tribes to sue entities misrepresenting their products. The Navajo Nation had registered "Navajo" as a trademark since 1943 and held 86 related trademarks by the time of the lawsuit.
In the latest issue of GQ, Brad Pitt discusses his role in "War Machine," a satirical film about General Stanley McChrystal's dismissal. When America faces trouble, it's because of our collective hubris. We believe we know best, but we shouldn't try to impose our views on others. We should set a positive example, much like a good father would." He adds, "We demonstrate our exceptional qualities through actions, not by forcing them on others." Reflecting further, Pitt mentions, "There was a film promoting itself in Europe, and the American flag was removed from posters because it wasn't seen as appealing. This shows how we've diminished our brand." When asked about his involvement in politics, Pitt says, "I can contribute in different ways. I can produce films with messages. No2x: Full Nodes Alex Morcos Blocked Unblock Follow Following Aug 14, 2017 Educate and inform the whole mass of the people... They are the only sure reliance for the preservation of our liberty. — Thomas Jefferson Liberty has never come from the government. Liberty has always come from the people. The history of liberty is a history of resistance. — Woodrow Wilson How crucial is it that running your own full Bitcoin node is easy? How important is it that, in the future, thousands or millions of Bitcoin nodes operate globally? If you wish to verify something personally rather than relying on a few websites, why should you run the verification code yourself? Is liberty and freedom safeguarded by institutional or corporate actions, or by the collective will of individuals? I find the idea that a handful of companies auditing the blockchain ensures its security as effectively as if each person audited it to be preposterous. Bitcoin exists to empower individual financial control. This control is essential when it conflicts with the state or large corporations. Why do web companies assist in censoring the internet in China? Why does the banking system restrict certain legal transactions? A limited number of companies cannot withstand state pressure nor is it in their best interest to do so. Running an individual node is already challenging, with actions measured in hours or days, and it will become more difficult as Bitcoin grows. For Bitcoin's success, we must foster a culture of individual responsibility. This does not mean everyone must run a full node, nor does it imply that using services or SPV forms of Bitcoin invalidates your participation. However, it does mean there must be numerous people running full nodes, and most importantly, there should not be significant barriers to doing so if that is your choice. Changing the blockchain or preserving Bitcoin's freedom will be impossible not just because a few companies claim to protect it, but because countless individuals enforce these principles. Does this mean the block size can never increase? Of course not. It simply means we should always prioritize caution and preserve this fundamental property—this is what Bitcoin uniquely offers the world. We should not lose it due to a potential Bitcoin startup's competition with PayPal.
Ledger Wallet stands out as one of the most secure Bitcoin wallets available. Given that Ledger Wallet CEO Eric Larchevêque keeps approximately 1,000 bitcoins—worth around $450,000 today—on his own wallet, this underscores its security. During an interview at CES, I discussed this with him. Ledger Wallet's initial product was a USB-like device secured with a PIN. If lost, the bitcoins remain safe since they require a private key for transfer. You can recover your wallet and move your bitcoins to a new one if needed. Essentially, Ledger Wallet increases security by combining multiple access factors, including a physical element. Even if your computer is compromised, hackers can't access your bitcoins because the critical code runs directly from the wallet. The company recently introduced the Ledger Blue, featuring a touchscreen and support for USB, Bluetooth, and NFC, while maintaining similar security mechanisms. When I asked Larchevêque about the current state of Bitcoin, he remains optimistic but believes it won't achieve the widespread adoption previously anticipated, which aligns with his substantial personal investment in the cryptocurrency.
The 100 Days Campaign of 1815 marked the final conflict of the Napoleonic Wars, culminating in the end of Napoleon Bonaparte's imperial aspirations. After escaping Elba, Bonaparte returned to a France weary of King Louis XVIII and reasserted his authority. With just 600 guards, he reached Grenoble, where the 5th Regiment failed to arrest him, prompting Bonaparte to famously declare, "Soldiers of the 5th, if you dare, shoot your emperor." His forces swelled as thousands rallied to his cause, leading to a notice at the Place Vendôme stating, "From Napoleon to Louis XVIII: There’s no need to send more troops—I have plenty." By March 19, the Bourbons fled to Belgium, and Bonaparte took power, preparing for the impending Allied assault. In two months, he amassed 280,000 men, augmented further. Although formidable, this force faced nearly a million Allied troops, including the Duke of Wellington's 110,000 and Field Marshal Blücher's 120,000. Recognizing the numerical disadvantage, Bonaparte swiftly invaded Belgium with 125,000 men, aiming to divide and conquer Wellington and Blücher's armies.

Opening my LA Times this morning, I found the business section's front-page headline: "Steep drop in jobless rate has some Obama critics questioning the figures." This struck me as remarkable. A single tweet from Jack Welch sparked a significant reaction, landing on the Times' front page. These individuals wield substantial influence over the news agenda. However, there might be an overlooked irony. While the Bureau of Labor Statistics (BLS) hasn't manipulated unemployment data, the monthly household survey can be volatile. September's employment increase might be genuine or just a statistical anomaly. Alternatively, a less noticed part of the recent report indicated that the BLS had undercounted jobs by 386,000 from April 2011 to March 2012. This undercount might still be occurring, as suggested by Karl Smith. If so, September's figures could be compensating for this past undercount. The chart shows a steady but modest recovery, aligning with the revised data. Thus, the irony is that if the BLS has been undercounting, the job situation appeared worse than it was during the first half of the year, harming President Obama. Consequently, the critics might be not just mistaken but also inverted in their perspective. Instead of being unfairly favorable to Obama, the BLS numbers might have been unfairly detrimental to him. The September spike corrected this, albeit too late to significantly benefit Obama.

A French court has recently upheld a 2012 ruling, finding Monsanto guilty of chemical poisoning. The case involves a French farmer, Paul Francois, who developed neurological issues after inhaling Monsanto’s Lasso weedkiller in 2004. The appellate court in Lyon confirmed that Monsanto is criminally responsible for the farmer’s intoxication and ordered full compensation. Monsanto plans to appeal the decision. According to Francois, he suffered memory loss, headaches, and stammering due to inadequate warning labels on the product. Monsanto ceased using Lasso in the U.S. several years ago for commercial reasons, though it was once their top-selling herbicide. In related news, Greece and Latvia have decided to ban Monsanto’s GM maize, joining Germany and Scotland. This move targets Monsanto’s MON810 GM Maize, the only GM crop currently allowed in the EU, requiring specific permission for cultivation. Concerns remain regarding the potential impact of the controversial TPP agreement, which could allow corporations like Monsanto to force their products onto markets despite existing regulations.

A congressional bill aimed at assisting homeowners impacted by the mortgage crisis garners broad bipartisan support, even amid a recent White House veto threat. However, the bill includes a provision, introduced in the Senate, that lacks direct relevance to housing loans: it mandates that credit card companies and electronic payment processors, including PayPal, submit annual transaction summaries to the IRS for merchants earning over $10,000 annually and processing more than 200 transactions. This provision, added without debate, is intended to offset the housing bailout's costs and is forecasted to generate around $9.8 billion in revenue over ten years through enhanced tax compliance and improved income reporting accuracy. Yet, during hearings with the House Small Business Committee, Chair Nydia Velásquez expressed concerns about the significant technical and financial hurdles these requirements pose for both banks and small business owners. She argued that the assumption of increased profits from electronic transactions is flawed, particularly given the impact of chargebacks, merchant discounts, and fees. Small business advocates and privacy groups raised additional issues, citing the potential for IRS audits and the retention of sensitive personal information. Despite these reservations, the housing relief bill is anticipated to pass with substantial support, likely bypassing thorough scrutiny due to its imminent return to the House for a simple vote.
According to the latest hacker intelligence report from security firm Imperva, hackers are targeting vulnerabilities in the widely-used PHP web application development platform, which poses a significant threat to many websites. This aligns with the established trend of attackers focusing on common third-party components for maximum return. PHP serves as an alternative to Microsoft's ASP technology and is predominantly utilized on Linux web servers. "Compromised hosts can be turned into botnet slaves, compromising the overall security and health of the internet," noted Amichai Shulman, CTO at Imperva. "Given that PHP powers over 80% of all websites, including major platforms like Facebook and Wikipedia, the impact of these attacks can be substantial. It's crucial for the security community to give this issue greater attention."

A law school graduate from the University of California, Berkeley, named Justin Alexander Teixeira, 25, was sentenced on Monday to four years of probation and monthly 16-hour stints at an animal shelter for beheading a chicken-sized exotic bird called Turk during a drunken incident in October 2012 at a Las Vegas Strip resort. Teixeira apologized to the state of Nevada and those affected by the death of the helmeted guineafowl. Security footage showed him and two fellow Berkeley students, Eric Cuellar and Hazhir Kargaran, laughing and chasing the bird before Teixeira killed it. Cuellar and Kargaran each pleaded guilty to reduced misdemeanor charges and received fines and community service. Teixeira, from Placerville, California, admitted to one felony charge of killing another person's animal, which spared him from facing additional felony charges that could have resulted in up to eight years in prison. Instead, he completed a 190-day prison boot camp at Indian Springs. His defense attorney argued for a lenient 18-month probation sentence, noting that the incident would haunt Teixeira for life. However, the judge imposed a stricter sentence, requiring Teixeira to fulfill his animal shelter commitment throughout his probation period.

A University of British Columbia undergraduate geography student, Iain Marjoribanks, reports that Airbnb significantly impacts Vancouver's rental housing stock. His study, guided by the B.C. Non-Profit Housing Association, revealed that approximately 77% of Airbnb’s revenue comes from hosts who do not reside in the units they list—Marjoribanks terms these “commercial” hosts, contrasting with Airbnb’s portrayal of itself as primarily accommodating “casual” hosts. Additionally, the top 10% of hosts, dubbed “mega hosts,” generate about 27% of Airbnb’s revenue by listing three or more properties. These hosts either convert single homes into hotels with individual rooms or rent multiple properties across the city. Airbnb disputes these findings; according to Aaron Zifkin, the country manager of Airbnb in Canada, the majority of listings are in people's primary residences, rented out occasionally. However, Marjoribanks’ research indicates that only 47% of hosts use their primary residences, suggesting that if Airbnb were removed from Vancouver, the rental vacancy rate could increase from 0.8% to up to 2%. Victoria is considering regulatory measures for Airbnb, but Marjoribanks points out that other cities like San Francisco and Portland have faced challenges with compliance rates of around 9.5% and 11%, respectively. He proposes that cities or the province should impose fines on Airbnb for violations, which would then be passed onto non-compliant hosts. Marjoribanks argues that those fully removing a house from the market year-round should compensate for the societal costs this imposes.

Trade Minister Andrew Robb and Chinese commerce minister Gao Hucheng will officiate the signing, marking a significant step in reducing tariff barriers for Australian businesses. Peter Arkell, chairman of the Australian Chamber of Commerce in Shanghai, hailed the agreement as a landmark deal. As China transitions to a consumption-driven economy, the agreement is timely, enhancing demand for high-quality Australian goods and services. TOWCESTER, ENGLAND - ON MARCH 24: Jockey Jamie Moore jogs around the track at Towcester racecourse on March 24, 2014. (Photo by Alan Crowhurst/Getty Images) Regular jogging has been associated with numerous health benefits, according to many studies. However, recent research indicates that excessive running might be linked to a reduced lifespan. (Photo by Alan Crowhurst/Getty Images) In a study presented by Dr. Martin Matsumura, co-director of the Cardiovascular Research Institute at Lehigh Valley Health Network, HealthDay reported that individuals with no exercise and those classified as high-mileage runners tend to live shorter lives compared to moderate runners. The exact reasons remain unclear. Dr. Matsumura stated, "Our research did not identify specific factors explaining these lifespan differences. We are still trying to determine the ideal amount of running for optimal health and longevity." The study examined data from over 3,800 male and female runners participating in the Masters Running Study, a web-based health and training survey for runners aged 35 and older. Approximately 70% of participants reported running more than 20 miles weekly, with an average age of 42 for high-mileage runners. Health-related data, including the use of painkillers and prescription medications, alongside cardiovascular risks, diabetes, hypertension, and family history of chronic illnesses, were analyzed. However, none of these factors fully explained the shorter lifespans observed in high-mileage runners. Dr. James O'Keefe, director of preventive cardiology at the Mid-American Heart Institute in Kansas City, suggested that while the exact reasons are unknown, excessive running might lead to wear and tear. He recommended a slow to moderate pace for about 2.5 hours per week, or roughly two to three times a week. Dr. Matsumura advised, "I wouldn't discourage people from running," while Dr. O'Keefe noted that if someone wants to run a marathon, they should do so once and cross it off their list, given the ongoing research into this relationship.
"Do you have faith?" Peter hesitated before responding, "I’m sorry?" The priest repeated the question straightforwardly. Peter paused, setting aside his cucumber sandwich, and replied, "Yes," before quietly making his way away. The question, though seemingly simple, caught them off guard. They had just spent a pleasant day at a friend's summer wedding in the English countryside and hadn't anticipated discussing their religious beliefs during a buffet line. Both Peter and I have complicated relationships with religion. He grew up as a Methodist Christian but became an atheist in his early twenties. As for me, I was born Muslim, a faith that posed challenges since I always dreamed of traveling and avoiding marriage. Considering this, when a local Samoan family invited us to a Catholic church service, we were cautious but decided to attend, seeing it as a chance to experience Samoan culture. Our host, Lena, assured us, "Don't worry if you're not Catholic; we welcome everyone." She then added, "There's a free lunch afterward." This reassured us, and we accepted the invitation. After breakfast, I rummaged through my bag for something appropriate to wear, packing a conservative outfit as usual. We climbed into Lena's van and headed to the church. During the service, I noticed Peter shifting beside me. For me, it was a delightful cultural experience, but for him, it brought back memories of his childhood and the confusion he felt about his faith. Despite the language differences, the sentiment was familiar. When he recognized the Lord's Prayer, he whispered along in English and joined in the Amen. Some traditions are more enduring than we realize. I observed the congregation kneeling, crossing themselves, praying, and singing. At one point, Lena, usually vivacious and confident, had tears streaming down her face. This reminded me that religion, despite its flaws, provides comfort and solace to many. In today's media-driven world, it's easy to believe religion causes division and hate, but I didn't feel any of that in that welcoming church. As we listened to the hymns, my thoughts drifted. Perhaps if I, standing in that small village in religious Samoa, could stand with those of differing beliefs, the rest of the world might follow. Then, I returned to reality and prepared for our lunch.

In January 2012, Microsoft is set to release a significant Patch Tuesday update, encompassing seven security bulletins that address eight distinct vulnerabilities. Typically, previous January updates have included just one or two bulletins. One critical bulletin focuses on fixing a remote code execution flaw in Media Player, while six "important" bulletins tackle issues such as the BEAST SSL vulnerability, information disclosure bugs, escalation of privilege problems, and an enhancement to SEHOP (Structured Exception Handler Overwrite Protection) technology. The "important" classification for the BEAST SSL issue remains contentious, given that Microsoft had already provided a workaround by suggesting the use of the unaffected RC4 cipher in SSL configurations. Despite initial plans to release a patch in December, testing revealed issues, leading to the delay. Security expert Paul Henry notes that despite the substantial attention surrounding "The Beast," no actual attacks have materialized, maintaining its "important" status. Meanwhile, Adobe and Oracle will also release updates on January 10 and 17, respectively, making this a particularly active month for patches. Microsoft's advance notice is available here.

Tesla CEO Elon Musk announced during an earnings call that the upcoming Model Y electric crossover will leverage much of the architecture from the recently launched Model 3, aiming to expedite its market release. This decision marks a shift from earlier statements where Musk had described the Model Y as "completely different" from previous Tesla models. Musk explained, "With guidance from my executive team, the Model Y will indeed utilize significant components from the Model 3 to speed up production." Previously, Musk had intended to discontinue the 12-volt battery system used in other Tesla vehicles, which could have reduced wiring and simplified manufacturing. While Musk confirmed that automation through robotics remains a priority, reusing the Model 3's architecture would be less costly given Tesla's tight margins. Many automakers adopt a common architecture for various models to enhance efficiency and reduce production costs. The Model Y will share numerous parts with the Model 3, which is currently in production, and incorporate some features from other Tesla models, such as the Model X's distinctive flic-gate doors. Production of the Model Y is scheduled to begin late 2019 or early 2020. The Model Y is crucial for Tesla's objective of producing one million vehicles by 2020, with the company having shipped 47,000 cars in the first half of 2017.
I'm continually impressed by the extensive release notes Unreal Engine publishes with each update. Epic Games incorporates numerous features in every release, making it easy for new ones to sometimes slip through the cracks. In the latest version (4.17), I noticed the addition of a spectator camera feature for VR, which renders one image for the VR headset and another for the screen. While the documentation for this feature is somewhat lacking, particularly as the link in the Unreal Engine release notes is currently broken, I managed to figure it out and thought I'd share what I learned. Here’s how you can set up a spectator camera in three simple steps: 1. **Create a 2D Scene Capture Component** – Think of this as the camera for the spectator. I attached mine to the character actor I was already using. 2. **Create a Render Target** – This acts as the storage for the image captured by the 2D scene capture component. 3. **Set Your Spectator Screen Mode and Texture** – Version 4.17 introduced improvements in displaying to the main viewport with options like showing just one eye and applying or not applying distortion. For our needs, we set it to a texture and grabbed the texture from the render target we created. That's it! Now you can move the 2D scene capture component around and get a non-head-mounted view of the gameplay. Keep in mind that this will slightly reduce your frame rate due to the additional image rendering per frame. Use this feature judiciously.

A report discreetly released on Christmas Eve indicates that Australia's emissions grew by about 1% in 2014-15 relative to the prior year. The December 2015 quarterly update on carbon emissions, covering data up to June 2015, revealed this increase with minimal fanfare. This report, which is part of Australia’s international emissions reporting, shows a 0.8% rise in emissions for the fiscal year 2014-15, with a 1.3% increase when land use and deforestation are included. In 2014-15, Australia emitted 549.3 mega-tonnes of carbon dioxide. The report highlights rises in electricity, stationary energy, transport, fugitive emissions, and industrial processes, while noting a significant decrease in agricultural emissions by 3.8%. Electricity generation saw a 3% increase, even as consumer demand stayed constant. Black coal production rose 1.4%, and brown coal generation surged by 9.7%. Wind and other renewable energy sources contributed 12.2% more than the previous year, though hydroelectric generation dropped by 30.3%. Electricity generation remained the primary source of emissions, making up 34% of total emissions in 2014-15. Professor Will Steffen from the Climate Council emphasized the urgency to reduce reliance on coal to align with global commitments. "If we're adding more to the atmosphere than the year before, we're moving in the wrong direction," he stated. "We need to cut emissions swiftly and transition away from fossil fuels rapidly, starting with coal."

Industry-funded flu vaccine studies are more likely to be published in high-impact journals and receive more citations compared to non-industry-funded studies, as reported in a survey of 274 studies by Italian researchers from the Cochrane Vaccine Field. The study also noted that better methodology had little effect on these outcomes. Furthermore, there was a scarcity of robust evidence showing the flu vaccine's efficacy. High-quality studies should ideally have greater visibility and citation frequency; however, the research found no correlation between study quality and these metrics. Instead, industry funding was the strongest predictor of a study's prominence. Most flu vaccine studies were of poor quality, with only 18% aligning their conclusions with their findings. "Access to prestigious journals is heavily influenced by the size of the sponsor," noted researcher Tom Jefferson. "Pharmaceutical companies purchase reprints, translations, and journal advertising," he added. Jefferson suggested journals disclose their funding sources. The researchers also observed that studies supporting vaccine effectiveness were generally of poorer design and had lower concordance rates compared to those that did not support effectiveness. Government-funded studies were less likely to conclude that the flu vaccine was effective compared to industry-funded studies.
During a January 10 confirmation hearing, Senator Jeff Sessions, nominee for Attorney General, was questioned by Senator John Kennedy (R-LA) about his views on the Second Amendment. Sessions affirmed that the right to bear arms is "historic" and constitutionally protected. Senator Kennedy responded, "In Louisiana, we believe love is the answer, but we also uphold our constitutional right to own a gun, just in case. Can you share your thoughts on the Second Amendment?" Sessions replied, "Yes, I believe the Second Amendment represents a personal right and a historic liberty for Americans. The Constitution explicitly protects this right alongside other fundamental freedoms. My stance on this is well-documented." Sessions also emphasized that the right to bear arms can be revoked for those who violate the law. He stated, "Individuals can lose their right to possess a firearm if they violate the law. For instance, carrying a gun during a crime can result in harsher penalties and convictions."
WHITE HOUSE, Tenn. (WZTV) - Garrett Preston, 19, survived a close call when his vape mod exploded in his pocket, resulting in severe burns to his stomach, knee, and leg. He now aims to warn others about the risks. In September of last year, Garrett was working his first full shift at Dunkin Donuts in White House around 10 p.m. When he attempted to make a sandwich, a burst of flame erupted from his left pocket, where he kept his car keys, phone, and an IPV 3 box mod used for vaping. Rushing to the back of the store, he found his keys and vaporizer smoldering. A co-worker notified another employee to call 911; Garrett was subsequently taken to Vanderbilt's Burn Center for treatment. He suffered second- and third-degree burns and remained hospitalized for 14 hours. His recovery took a month and a week, during which no skin graft was necessary. Garrett believes the explosion occurred because the vape box lacked its protective back cover, and his keys might have triggered the battery. He warns that even though vape batteries are typically insulated, any cuts in the insulation could pose a risk. The screws had fallen off, preventing him from adjusting the wattage, but he had been using the device without issues for five to six months. Despite his injuries, Garrett continues to vape, seeing it as a safer alternative to smoking traditional cigarettes. Many of his friends have used vapes without incident, making him feel "unlucky" to be the one involved in an accident. "These stories about vape explosions often stem from user error," he says, hoping his experience will serve as a cautionary tale for others.

The Federal Reserve surprised many when its Open Market Committee decided at 2 p.m. not to reduce its $85 billion monthly bond purchases due to economic concerns. This announcement sparked an immediate market rally, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average jumping 141 points within minutes to close up 147 points by the end of the trading day. From a more critical standpoint, while others celebrate, I view it differently. The Fed's decision not to taper stimulus signals their inability to do so, given the current economic conditions. Bernanke and the Fed recognize that halting quantitative easing (QE) would lead to higher interest rates, a stronger dollar, deflation, and a stock market and real estate market collapse, ultimately causing the U.S. economy to falter. Despite the Fed's attempts to prevent these outcomes, the laws of economics are immutable. These effects will occur regardless of the Fed’s actions. The modest market reaction of only 150 points suggests the situation may be more fragile than anticipated. The Fed clearly intends to continue supporting the market for as long as possible. A major oversight among market participants is the belief that the Fed controls the markets; this is far from true. The market's potential reversal tomorrow highlights the uncertainty and volatility ahead.

Excessive medical intervention is a significant factor in post-heart attack fatalities. Aggressive treatments like angioplasty and thrombolytic drugs, when administered outside their optimal timeframes, can more than double the risk of patient mortality. Researchers from the Quebec Healthcare Assessment Agency in Montreal found that timely administration of these treatments is crucial. Interventions such as angioplasty should occur within 90 minutes of hospital admission, while thrombolytic drugs should be given within 30 minutes. However, 68% of angioplasties and 54% of thrombolytic administrations occurred after these critical windows. Consequently, patients receiving delayed treatments had a mortality rate nearly twice as high as those treated promptly. (Source: Journal of the American Medical Association, 2010; 303: 2148).

When did laws start requiring accompanying press releases from professional PR firms, complete with photos of the sponsoring state officials? This practice seems shameless. Yet, it drew attention to a groundbreaking law in Washington State that bans "malicious" RFID spying. Effective in July, this pioneering legislation will charge anyone caught remotely scanning a person without their knowledge and consent, for fraudulent or illegal purposes, with a Class C felony. While this addresses obvious criminal misuse, the original bill also proposed an opt-in measure for retailers and others to track your activity using various identification methods like discount cards, implants, or credit cards—methods that were later removed due to strong corporate lobbying. The opt-in provision was dropped from the final bill. Interested parties can find more details in the provided link.
It's impossible to capture a direct image of a Higgs boson due to its minuscule size and fleeting nature. However, physicists can visualize its counterparts, such as atomic structures, enhancing our knowledge of atomic physics. Recently, they achieved a remarkable feat: the first-ever image of a single atom’s shadow. This is the smallest object observable with visible light. A team headed by Dave Kielpinski from Griffith University in Australia managed to trap a single ytterbium ion using an electric field, allowing it to be positioned for imaging. They illuminated the ion with light of a particular frequency and utilized an ultra-high-resolution microscope to capture the shadow cast on a CCD detector. The experiment confirmed that a single atom can indeed produce a shadow. This breakthrough also holds practical value, such as aiding in the determination of suitable light types for observing biological processes without causing damage. The findings were published this week in Nature Communications.
Since its inception, Mozilla has aimed to empower users across various platforms and devices. One of our experiments was Firefox Home, which sought to bring parts of the Firefox experience to iOS, particularly focusing on Firefox Sync. While this project offered valuable insights and experience with the platform, we have chosen to remove Firefox Home from the Apple App Store and redirect our resources to other initiatives. For anyone interested in continuing to use or enhance the iOS Sync client used by Firefox Home, the source code is now available on GitHub, without Mozilla trademarks, enabling independent development. As with all Mozilla projects, developers are encouraged to adhere to our trademark policies. We remain dedicated to delivering compelling user experiences on as many platforms and devices as possible and will continue to explore the best approaches to offer great experiences to iOS users. – mconnor, on behalf of the Firefox and Services teams

A red flag that a company may be in trouble is when its CEO becomes defensive against any critique of his vague assurances. These CEOs frequently attempt to intimidate analysts by projecting unwavering confidence to mask uncomfortable truths. "I succeeded before," they often argue, "so I will succeed again." This translates to, "Just accept my word without questioning me." In contrast, CEOs with nothing to hide tend to be forthcoming and detailed in their explanations. They recognize the analyst's role in skepticism and are willing to provide ample information. During the second presidential debate, when moderator Candy Crowley questioned Romney's budget figures, his defensive reply echoed the behavior of a CEO whose company is on shaky ground. Given the ambitious claims Romney made about his budget—reducing taxes, closing loopholes, avoiding increased deficits, boosting growth, and increasing military spending—it's unsurprising that many Americans remain doubtful. The question then arises: What gives Romney such confidence that his budget numbers hold? Why won't he disclose more details to the public? If Romney were a public-company CEO, many savvy investors would deduce that his reluctance to share the numbers indicates they don’t add up. Below is the video clip: Candy Crowley: "...If somehow the numbers don't add up, would you be willing to look again at a 20% (tax cut)?" Mitt Romney: "Well of course they add up. I was someone who ran businesses for 25 years and balanced the budget. I ran the Olympics and balanced the budget. Recently, I came across a stepped-triangular (ST) approximation that can be realized through a cascade of recursive running sum (RRS) filters. Here’s a brief introduction to the stepped-triangular approximation. Introduced by Jovanovic-Dolecek and Mitra [1], this method provides a quantized version of a low-pass filter (LPF) impulse response. Figure 1 illustrates this approximation. [Figure 1: Stepped Approximation of a LPF Impulse] The original paper [1] outlines how to derive the stepped-triangular approximation for an LPF impulse response, leading to a transfer function represented as a cascade of recursive sums. Specifically, \(H_{\text{rrs}}(z)\) denotes the familiar recursive running sum, which produces a flat impulse response. [Figure 2: Impulse Response Recursive Running Sum] \(H_{\text{rss}}(z)\) represents a recursive sparse sum, utilizing every \(s\) samples. [Figure 3: Impulse Response Recursive Sparse Sum] Both can be efficiently implemented with an integrator and difference combination. To create the stepped-triangular approximation from an LPF impulse response, a simple rounding function \(g[n]\) is applied. This function quantizes \(h[n]\) into one of three stepped-triangular approximation types. Figures 1 and 4 demonstrate one such example, where the number of peak samples can be less than, equal to, or greater than \(s\), with \(s\) being the number of samples per step. [Figure 4: Frequency Response for an ST-Approximation] Figure 4 showcases the frequency response for an ST-approximation, comparing the original response (blue) with the approximation using the stepped-triangular approach (red). The reader can judge the quality of this approximation based on its computational efficiency. [1] Jovanovic-Dolecek, G., & Mitra, S., “Design of FIR Lowpass Filters Using Stepped Triangular Approximation”
In 2017, Netflix released at least 34 original movies, and it aims to produce 80 in 2018, according to chief content officer Ted Sarandos. This number surpasses the output of most major movie studios. For instance, Disney, Warner Bros., and Sony collectively released 64 movies in 2016 and 2017, respectively, compared to Netflix's proposed 80. Major US studios have seen a decline in their output since the "golden age of Hollywood," producing fewer but bigger releases due to competition from TV and streaming services, along with rising ticket prices. Countries like India continue to produce more feature films than the US, though the US remains dominant in box office revenue. To fund its ambitious movie release strategy, Netflix plans to increase its content budget to between $7-8 billion in 2018, up from $6 billion in 2017. The 2018 lineup includes both low-budget acquisitions and high-profile productions, such as Martin Scorsese's The Irishman. Netflix concluded 2017 with its most expensive film to date, Bright, which reportedly cost $90 million. Notably, several of Netflix's recent releases demonstrated potential for box office success, yet they were not distributed theatrically. This allows Netflix more flexibility to experiment, unlike traditional studios with fixed theatrical release schedules and larger marketing budgets.

Approximately 2 million children stand to lose health coverage starting next month unless Congress renews funding for the Children's Health Insurance Program (CHIP) by Friday, according to a recent report. State administrators report rising panic among parents, with permanent damage already inflicted on the program aimed at uninsured children. CHIP currently insures nearly 9 million children nationwide. Without funding, some states have begun advising parents to secure their children's final medical care while funds last. "We're getting countless calls from worried families," said Cathy Caldwell, director of Alabama's Bureau of Children's Health Insurance. "It's extremely stressful." Experts find this situation perplexing, given CHIP's widespread popularity and bipartisan support. On Wednesday, Democrats urged Republicans to act by Friday. "This is the worst Christmas scenario," Rep. Jackie Speier, D-Calif., remarked during a press event. The report indicates that 20 states will exhaust their CHIP funds by early 2018. A temporary measure allocates $1.24 billion, benefiting states with more urgent needs but at the expense of others. This shift delays financial strain for some states but accelerates it for others. All states will face funding shortages by February.
Thousands of individuals burdened by private student loan debt might receive relief, as at least $5 billion in such debt lacks critical documentation necessary for creditors to enforce payments. According to reports from The New York Times, creditors have faced obstacles in court when attempting to collect on past debts, due to insufficient ownership records and flawed documentation. One of the largest holders of private student loan debt, the National Collegiate Student Loan Trusts, has struggled to provide legal paperwork proving ownership of over $5 billion in student loans. Consequently, many debts have been dismissed in court. For instance, Samantha Watson, a 33-year-old mother of three, had $31,000 in student loans pursued through the courts. However, her debt was erased when a judge found that National Collegiate failed to establish the chain of title on the loans. Similar dismissals have occurred in several other court cases across Texas, New Hampshire, and Ohio.

She rejected Republican calls for focusing exclusively on spending reductions and entitlement reforms. Pelosi argued that the U.S. faces a budget deficit issue rather than just a spending problem. "We have a budget deficit problem that we need to address," she stated during an interview with Fox News. She also emphasized that the deficit and debt levels are "immoral" and must be reduced. Pelosi supported a comprehensive plan aimed at ending subsidies for large oil companies and eliminating certain tax breaks. She contended that these tax subsidies generate more revenue for the U.S. Treasury than additional spending cuts, which could harm critical areas like education. "Investment in education is what brings the most money to the Treasury," she noted, adding that cuts in education and scientific research are detrimental and are currently being affected by sequestration. Both Democrats and Republicans seek to avoid the sequestration, which entails automatic cuts to military and non-defense programs, due to take effect on March 1 if no action is taken. While Republicans advocate for spending cuts alone, Democrats propose a combination of cuts and increased revenue. President Obama warned on Saturday that sequestration would severely impact middle-class families and the economy. His administration warned that the cuts would affect law enforcement, education programs, and mental health services, leading to furloughs for thousands of government workers. Republicans countered that new tax revenues were included in the January fiscal cliff agreement and urged the president to target spending cuts and entitlement reforms.

This acquisition provides First Solar with access to a 1,100 MW pipeline of solar projects in the southwestern United States and control over additional NextLight projects totaling 570 MW with signed power purchase agreements. Airport lounges can be accessed by faking a QR code, as demonstrated by computer security expert Przemek Jaroszewski. He developed a 600-line JavaScript program to generate a QR code for "Batholemew Simpson," a fictional business-class traveler, when his own boarding card was rejected by an automated reader. Jaroszewski successfully used this method to gain entry to multiple lounges across Europe, exploiting the lack of cross-checking between lounge entry systems and aircraft passenger manifests. Ten years earlier, computer science student Chris Soghoian created a boarding card generator to bypass airport security, leading to an FBI visit. Today, US aviation checkpoints verify boarding cards using a TSA cryptographic signature, preventing such hacks from granting unauthorized access to lounges but still allowing ticketed passengers entry. Jaroszewski refrains from releasing his code due to fears of an FBI investigation, though recreation of the hack is reportedly straightforward. His methods highlight ongoing security vulnerabilities in boarding pass systems.

This criticism came after a nationalist march in Kaunas, Lithuania's second-largest city, took place earlier this month. Participants carried portraits of Juozas Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis, a pro-Nazi former ruler who aided German forces in sending 30,000 Jews to their deaths. The marchers also displayed signs stating "Lithuania for Lithuanians." Efraim Zuroff, from the center's Israel office, informed JTA that the annual ultra-nationalist march saw a significant increase in participants—1,000 this year compared to 600 last year—attributing it to the government's role in Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis' 2012 reburial in Kaunas, which was funded by the Lithuanian government. Previously, his remains were buried in Putnam, Connecticut, USA. He gained recognition in Lithuania in 2009 when President Valdas Adamkus awarded him the Grand Cross of the Order of Vytautas the Great for his government's role in restoring Lithuanian statehood after Soviet occupation. SnapPea is a free Windows utility that enables you to manage apps, music, photos, and SMS messages on your Android phone from your desktop. Think of it as an "iTunes for Android," but without the poor performance. If you're seeking an all-in-one solution to manage your Android phone, SnapPea could be worth trying. Its primary appeal is its comprehensive approach; it provides a single application to handle all aspects of your phone. Connect your phone to your PC via USB (with USB debugging enabled) or pair it with the app over Wi-Fi. Once connected, you can manage, organize, and transfer files between your Android device and the app's interface. You can install apps directly, organize and update contacts, back up your phone, manage photos, music, and videos, or even import iTunes playlists to sync your music. Notably, SnapPea also lets you send and receive SMS messages directly from your desktop through your Android phone. SnapPea faces competition from AirDroid, another utility with its own advantages. AirDroid operates through a web browser, making it more versatile across different platforms, while SnapPea offers additional functionalities that AirDroid lacks. Ultimately, which tool works best depends on your specific needs. SnapPea is free for Windows and available for download. Unfortunately, there’s no version for macOS or Linux, but Mac users can sideload SnapPea's APK installer if needed.
Stephen Paddock amassed a substantial arsenal before carrying out the deadliest mass shooting in modern U.S. history in Las Vegas. This event, along with the sheer volume of Paddock's weapons, has once again brought gun control to the forefront of the U.S. national discourse. How prevalent is multiple gun ownership among Americans? And who holds these firearms? Here’s a look at gun ownership patterns in America: Do many Americans own guns? Absolutely. The United States leads the world in gun ownership, possessing roughly half of all global firearms despite comprising only 5% of the world's population. In terms of per capita ownership, the U.S. has the highest rate. As of 2013, there were an estimated 89 to 100 guns for every 100 Americans—equivalent to nearly one firearm per person. Serbia ranks second with 75.6 guns per 100 people. Who owns these guns? According to data from the General Social Survey and Gallup in 2013, only about a quarter of Americans own firearms. However, many gun owners possess multiple weapons. On average, an American gun owner owns around three firearms, based on a 2015 survey by Harvard and Northwestern University. Over half of gun owners have just one or two guns, while 14%—amounting to 7.7 million people or 3% of the U.S. population—own between eight and 140 guns. This 3% alone accounts for half of all civilian firearms in the country. The police discovered 47 guns linked to Paddock in three locations two days post-shooting, placing him within this 3%. It's worth noting that gun ownership surveys may include inaccuracies. Given that many U.S. states allow firearm purchases without permits or licenses, there isn't a comprehensive system for tracking gun owners.

Emojis and stickers are increasingly integral to our daily communication, and Google has introduced a new Allo feature that converts selfies into custom cartoon stickers. Unlike other avatar systems such as Nintendo’s Miis or Snapchat’s Bitmoji, Google’s approach leverages machine learning and neural networks to map facial features onto artist-designed avatars created by Lamar Abrams. These avatars can be further customized, and the result is a range of cartoon stickers representing you that can be used in future Allo conversations. Google’s research blog details their efforts to avoid the uncanny valley and ensure diverse representation across various races, genders, and age groups. This new selfie-to-sticker tool is now available for Allo on Android, with an iOS release expected shortly.

Many individuals, particularly those in the media, focus intensely on whether hybrid, plug-in hybrid, or electric vehicles will "pay for themselves" through fuel savings. An example of this is the misleading claim that "the payoff for plug-in hybrids: 95 years?" Sometimes, it is explicitly stated that a particular car will save money, and it makes sense to scrutinize such claims. However, the prevailing narrative has evolved into a broader belief that the primary goal of environmentally friendly transportation is financial savings, and all new green cars are judged by this standard. This perspective is absurd. Have we ever insisted that cars should always pay for themselves? When someone opts for a larger V6 engine, sunroof, or fancy wheels, do we question whether these choices will "pay for themselves"? Why is it acceptable for people to make aesthetic or performance-based decisions without justification, yet those choosing cleaner cars for reasons like reduced emissions, enjoyment of electric driving, or support for new clean technology are asked to provide a financial rationale? While additional costs for more efficient vehicles can indeed reduce expenses over time, a powerful engine merely incurs higher upfront and operational costs. Let us abandon the financial double standard for greener cars unless the sole concern truly is cost-effectiveness.
Jakob de Haan, the head of research at De Nederlandsche Bank (DNB), recently addressed questions about bitcoin's role in global finance during a speech at the reinvent.money conference on September 26th. Despite some unusually candid remarks given his position, de Haan responded to queries that criticized the euro as a "bullshit fiat currency." In a newly released video, de Haan acknowledged that a few individuals believe digital currencies will be the future, stating, "Let's wait and see. I'm not opposed to these initiatives, and if you're correct, the world will look very different, but by then I'll likely be retired." He also admitted he is not an expert in bitcoin but noted that the central bank is closely monitoring the technology and conducting research on it. De Haan clarified that DNB is not fundamentally opposed to alternative digital currencies and has taken steps to inform the public that digital currencies lack the consumer protections offered by traditional ones. Addressing concerns about competition from private currencies, de Haan highlighted bitcoin's volatility against fiat currencies as a barrier to broader adoption. He concluded by emphasizing that central banks aim to maintain price stability for ordinary people.

While discussions often focus on Obamacare's exchange enrollment falling short of CBO's 2010 projections, another set of predictions from the CBO is equally noteworthy. Initially, they forecasted a reduction of 8 million individuals in private insurance coverage by 2016. However, this did not materialize. Instead, thanks to the individual mandate encouraging more people to buy private insurance and the employer mandate prompting greater employer coverage, private insurance saw an increase of over 16 million by mid-2015. A chart illustrates this shift; private coverage, which had oscillated around 61% of the population, surged to 66% within a year. Economic recovery likely contributed somewhat, but the significant change occurred in Q4 2013 with the implementation of Obamacare. In summary, while exchange enrollment fell short by about 7 million, Obamacare outperformed on private insurance by 24 million. This outcome is positive, as it means more people are insured through their employers, which provides better coverage and reduces taxpayer expenses. This success story, while underreported, is substantial.

"I knew my interpreter saved lives," said Boisvert. "Now, I can't protect him." The deadline for applying for 1,000 new visas looms on December 31, with around 13,000 visas issued since 2009. Representative James McGovern of Worcester is proposing to extend the deadline and increase the number of visas. An Afghan interpreter in Boston, who assisted both Marines, described the peril faced by interpreters and their families, noting one interpreter was beheaded. McGovern emphasized the moral imperative to act, given the interpreters' vulnerability. His proposal is included in the National Defense Authorization Act for 2015 under consideration by the U.S. Senate. With 9,000 applicants and only 4,000 visas granted this year, extending the deadline and adding more visas, McGovern argued, would be a significant step forward.
Image copyright AFP Fiat Chrysler's shares dropped significantly on the Milan stock exchange following media reports that it might face a sales ban in Germany over alleged emissions test cheating. Shares initially declined by up to 5% in early trading but later recovered somewhat. Last weekend, Germany's Bild newspaper reported that the country’s regulators had uncovered emissions-cheating software in Fiat vehicles. This comes after Volkswagen faced a similar scandal. According to Bild, the German Federal Motor Vehicle Office (KBA) detected software in a Fiat car that deactivated emissions controls after just 22 minutes—a period shorter than a standard test duration. The KBA reportedly informed the European Commission and Italian authorities about these findings. Fiat Chrysler has yet to comment on these allegations and has postponed a scheduled meeting with German authorities, citing Italian responsibility for vehicle testing. German Transport Minister Alexander Dobrindt has criticized Fiat’s “uncooperative” stance, describing it as “totally incomprehensible.”

The Royal College of Nursing (RCN) has called for a dedicated law to address verbal and physical abuse against nurses treating the public. Every day, 193 attacks on UK healthcare workers are reported, and NHS Protect data shows a 4% increase in physical assaults from 67,864 in 2014/15 to 70,555 in 2015/16. An RCN survey revealed that 56% of members had experienced physical or verbal abuse from patients, while 63% faced such abuse from patients' relatives or members of the public. A parliamentary debate will now take place, with MPs considering whether to criminalize attacks on public healthcare professionals. Over 116,000 people signed a petition initiated by LBC radio presenter Nick Ferrari. Advocates hope a specific law would enhance deterrence for those who verbally or physically assault nurses. Each year, these incidents cost the health service approximately £69 million due to staff absences, reduced productivity, and heightened security needs. In response to the petition, the government stated that assaults against NHS staff are already criminalized, with public-facing victims receiving more serious treatment. However, RCN Professional Lead for Primary and Community Care, Kathryn Yates, remains deeply concerned about the safety of lone-working nurses in the community. Since 2015, half of all community nurses reported feeling vulnerable at work, with nearly half citing worsening risks due to NHS budget cuts. Increased caseloads and staffing shortages mean many nurses must work alone. Some local councils have slashed community nursing budgets by 50%, and there has been a 42% reduction in district nurses. The shortage of general practice nurses and nurse practitioners exacerbates the workforce crisis in primary care, adding to the risks faced by lone workers.
Over 500 school districts across the country have committed to correctly pronouncing every student's name due to a campaign by leftist education groups that views mispronunciation as a "microaggression." This pledge aims to address the anxiety and resentment caused by incorrect pronunciations, aligning with a broader list of "microaggressions" that include everyday interactions perceived as discriminatory. The initiative, known as "My Name, My Identity: A Declaration of Self," was initiated by the National Association for Bilingual Education and a California school district. It encourages teachers to share their name stories on social media and celebrate cultural diversity. Rita Kohli, an education professor at the University of California, Riverside, argued that mispronouncing names can impede the learning process, reflecting the family and cultural background of students. The campaign's materials emphasize the importance of respecting students' names and identities.
Romania's Prime Minister Sorin Grindeanu announced on Saturday that the government intends to revoke a corruption decree that has ignited the country's largest protests since Nicolae Ceaușescu's regime fell in 1989. "We will convene a special cabinet meeting tomorrow to rescind this decree," Grindeanu stated during a press conference. "I do not wish to divide Romania; it must remain united." Grindeanu also scheduled an urgent cabinet meeting for Sunday to explore a legal pathway ensuring the decree does not come into force. Approximately 120,000 demonstrators participated in protests across Romania for the fifth consecutive day, opposing the measure that decriminalized minor official misconduct. On Friday, demonstrations drew between 200,000 and 250,000 people nationwide. The move was anticipated, given that Grindeanu’s Social Democratic Party leader had hinted earlier on Saturday that the government might retract the decree. Grindeanu emphasized that the decree would not be enforced. Meanwhile, Calin Tariceanu, leader of the ALDE party and a junior coalition partner, suggested that the decision was based on the principle of “nothing to hide.” The Constitutional Court is set to rule next week on the legality of the decree. It aimed to decriminalize certain abuse-of-power offenses and redefine conflicts of interest, potentially allowing public officials to favor business partners in contract decisions. Grindeanu’s administration, which took office just over a month ago, also plans to release about 2,500 prisoners serving sentences under five years. Critics argue that these measures were intended to release thousands of political figures implicated in anti-corruption efforts, many of whom belong to the Social Democratic Party. The PSD regained power after winning elections in December, promising to increase salaries and pensions in Europe's second-poorest country.

How to boost profits from misprinted money by James Allum on August 26, 2016 in Editorial The collectors' market for misprinted money vividly illustrates the value of errors in the complex minting process. A wide range of potential mistakes can occur, from upside-down designs to misaligned images. The rarer the errors, the higher the value. Let's explore some notable examples of US currency mishaps and their inflated values. **$1 Inverted Obverse** An eagle-eyed collector will notice the back of this $1 bill is upside down, with about 25% of the iconic image cut off. The design appears almost satanic with the inverted eagle's eye pointing downwards. Value: $1,053 Increase: 105,200% **$1 Foldover** This bill shows George Washington seemingly blowing his nose due to a foldover before cutting. The top right corner is bent, revealing the serial number, treasury seal, and two of the four district numbers. Value: $760.50 Increase: 75,950% **$20 Star Shift** A subtle error where a star near Andrew Jackson's head has shifted to the right, along with the serial numbers, treasury seal, and district numbers. Value: $643 Increase: 3,115% **$1 Multiple Gutter Folds** Unexpected crimps create random lines on the bill, reminiscent of a laser show. This may indicate George Washington enjoyed Pink Floyd concerts. Value: $175.50 Increase: 1,7450% **$10 Missing Face Printing** Clarence, the mint worker, fell asleep, resulting in a faceless $10 bill. Despite the missing portrait, other details remain intact. Value: $351 Increase: 3,050% **$20 Missing Back Printing** This time, Clarence was fired for a similar mistake, leaving the back of the bill blank. Value: $555.75 Increase: 2,678.75% **$10 Ink Smear** A disgruntled ex-employee left an ink smear on the bill, making it resemble a squid's ink explosion. Value: $438.75 Increase: 4,287.5% All images and values courtesy of www.stacksbowers.com
